A High Dimensional Wild Bootstrap Max-Test for Detecting the Presence
of Significant Predictors
Jonathan B. Hill
Dept. of Economics, University of North Carolina, Chapel Hill
Department of Economics, University of North Carolina, Chapel Hill, North
Carolina, E-mail:
jbhill@email.unc.edu
;
https://tarheels.live/jbhill/
. We kindly acknowledge Min Qian for
providing the R code developed for the ART procedure in
McKeague and Qian (
2015
)
.
(
This draft:
)
Abstract
We construct a block bootstrap max-test for detecting the presence of
significant predictors in a high dimensional setting, allowing for weakly
dependent and heterogeneous (possibly non-stationary) data. The number of
covariates to be screened may be large
p
p
>>
>>
n
n
, and growing at an
exponential rate, provided
ln
‚Ā°
(
p
)
\ln(p)
=
=
o
‚Äč
(
n
a
)
o(n^{a})
for some
a
a
>
>
0
that depends on memory decay and the growth of higher moments. We study the
problem of correlation screening in a high dimensional marginal regression
setting, assuming so-called
physical dependence
in a time series
setting. We entirely sidestep covariance matrix estimation and adaptive
re-sampling by working with a max-statistic over the many computed
parameters. Thus we do not need endogenous selection of the most relevant
predictor index yielding non-uniform asymptotics, nor do we need a
post-estimation Bonferroni correction. The non-standard limit distribution
arising from the maximum of an increasing number of estimators is easily
approximated by a multiplier (wild) block bootstrap. The max-test controls
for size well, performs well against various deviations from the null,
including very slight deviations with a weak or sparse signal. A numerical
experiment is performed and an empirical example with the VIX volatility
index is provided.
Key words and phrases
: correlation learning, marginal screening,
high dimension, physical dependence.
MSC classifications
: 62F07, 62H15, 62G10.
1
Introduction
The selection of statistically significant predictors and subsequent
inference is a central challenge in applied sciences, while recent advances
in high dimensional settings pose new challenges
(e.g. Fan and Li,
2006
)
.
High dimensional data sets are now common due to the amount of data
available, the sophisticated nature of data collection methods, and the use
of machine learning and related nonparametric methods for estimation of
unknown link functions. This covers many scientific fields, including
economics, finance, engineering, genetics, communication and meteorology,
and other material sciences
(e.g. Buhlmann and van¬†de
Geer,
2011
; Fan et¬†al.,
2011
; Belloni
et¬†al.,
2014
)
.
Historically multiple testing and related post-estimation p-value bounding
are exploited
(e.g. Benjamini and
Hochberg,
1995
; Efron,
2006
; McCloskey,
2017
,
2020
,
2024
)
. Conversely,
McKeague and Qian (
2015
)
propose a single test for detecting
the most significant predictor (if there is one), while controlling for the
family-wise error rate
[
fwer
]. They work in an iid
setting, with methods ultimately akin to those developed in
Andrews and Cheng (
2012
,
2013
,
2014
)
and
Cheng (
2015
)
for weakly identified models, and while the number of covariates
p
p
>
>
n
n
is allowed, where
n
n
is the sample size,
p
p
is otherwise
fixed
, thus
p
/
n
‚Üí
0
p/n\rightarrow 0
yielding eventually a low dimension
setting. Ostensibly a post estimation framework with
fwer
control
in a true high dimensional setting
p
/
n
p/n
‚Üí
\rightarrow
‚ąě
\infty
is not yet
available. Related work for independent data with fixed
p

includes

(Zhang and Laber,

(Tang et¬†al.,

.

We extend the marginal screening framework to a

, under weak dependence and heterogeneity assumptions. The

we allow for time series or cross sections with non-iid data.
1
1
1
Our proposed method and theory also allow for
both
time series and
cross-sections in a panel. High dimensionality here is also naturally
affected by fixed effects dummies. We do not, however, expand notation to
include this case for the sake of clarity.
We do so
pre-estimation
by avoiding the endogenous selection of an optimal covariate index
altogether. Indeed, it is unnecessary for the purpose of a test. Likewise,
we sidestep sequential testing and therefore avoid the need for
fwer
control, contrary to
Benjamini and
Hochberg (
1995
)
,
Dudoit et¬†al. (
2003
)
,
Efron (
2006
)
and others, and contrary to
McKeague and Qian (
2015
)
who
control

with a single test by using a nuisance sequence
őĽ
n
\lambda_{n}
. The latter is selected in practice by using a second
bootstrap procedure. We use a single test where a single dependent bootstrap
naturally yields an asymptotic level
őĪ
\alpha
test, without the need for a
nuisance sequence. Working pre-estimation also allows us to sidestep
estimating a full HD model under sparsity. The latter is computationally
intensive, may be unstable or fragile
(e.g. Koles√°r et¬†al.,
2024
)
, and
ultimately potentially
illusory
: sparsity may not be appropriate or
feasible in many economic and finance settings
(see,
e.g. Giannone et¬†al.,
2021
)
.
Reduced dimension regression models in the high dimensional parametric
statistics and machine learning literatures are variously called
marginal regression
,

, and
sure

(e.g. Fan and Lv,
2008
; Genovese et¬†al.,

)
, and includes
canonical correlation analysis
(e.g. McKeague and Zhang,
2022
)
.

A max-test alleviates the need for adaptive re-sampling via non-parametric
bootstrap, although inference can be easily gained by multiplier (wild)
block bootstrap
(cf. Hill,
2025b
)
. The latter allows for
asymptotically correct inference without prior knowledge of the limit
distribution. Furthermore, a max-test can yield amplified power against
small or weak deviations from the null amounting to a sparse signal
(see,
e.g., Hill,
2025b
; Hill and Li,
2025
)
, in particular at small sample sizes.
See Section
5
. We do not explore an endogenously selected
optimal covariate index
l
^
n
\hat{l}_{n}
under weak dependence, leaving that
for future work.
Historically, of course, there is broad interest in an endogenously selected
‚Äú
most informative
‚ÄĚ regressor, or
generally (adaptive) post-model-selection inference. Indeed, selecting an
optimal predictor can be performed forward-stepwise to generate a subsequent
regression model. See, e.g.,
Leeb and P√∂tscher (
2006
)
for deep results, and
consult
Laber and Murphy (
2011
)
,
Berk et¬†al. (
2013
)
,
McKeague and Qian (

)
and
McCloskey (

,

,

)
for further reading.
The present paper extends work in
Hill (

)
, who uses low
dimensional models with a fixed dimensional nuisance covariate to test a

p

‚Üí

‚Üí

.
Hill (

)
imposes an iid assumption in a linear regression
framework, yielding four covariate tail cases by invoking
Nemirovski (

‚Äôs inequality (bounded, sub-Gaussian, sub-exponential,
‚Ąí

(exponential or polynomial). Nuisance covariates, however, are typically
ignored in a correlation screening setting. Further, weak dependence
evidently forces us to work in a now standard sub-exponential tail setting,
hence we rely on Bernstein‚Äôs inequality for HD arguments leading to an
exponential bound on
p
n
p_{n}
.
Weak dependence is a key requirement if high dimensionality is due to the
nuances of large panel models with a time dimension, including many
qualitative dummies, and (multi-way) fixed effects
(Angrist and Hahn,
2004
; Belloni
et¬†al.,
2014
; Correia,
2016
)
. As far as
we know, time series and panels have not been studied in the correlation
learning or sure screening literature. Indeed, compared to
McKeague and Qian (
2015
)
,
Zhang and Laber (

)
,
Tang et¬†al. (

)
, and
Hill (

)
, we deliver a complete theory for arbitrarily
weighted statistics

5
Simulation Study
We now study the small sample properties of the proposed test.
McKeague and Qian (
2015
)
compare their
adaptive resampling test
[ART]
with the Likelihood Ratio test, multiple testing with Bonferroni correction,
centered percentile bootstrap, and higher criticism. They find that in an
iid linear regression setting ART has competitive empirical power and
accurate size overall, and generally dominates all other methods. We
therefore compare our test with ART based on a nonparametric bootstrap and
parametric wild bootstrap.

\mathcal{R}_{[\alpha n]}
. This
yields the double bootstrap as detailed in
McKeague and Qian (
2015
)
.
5.3
Results
Rejection frequencies at the
5
%
5\%
level are shown in Figures G.1-G.10 in
the supplemental material

, Appendix

. Each test (max, ave, ART) works monotonically better as
cross-covariate dependence increases, through mutual (in)dependence with
ő≥
\gamma
‚ąą
\in
{
0
,
.5
,
.8
}
\{0,.5,.8\}
and AR feedback. Indeed, under AR covariates
C2 the tests perform similarly, with mildly low size under
H
0
H_{0}
, and
empirical power at or near
100
%
100\%
under each
H
1
H_{1}
. We therefore only
report results under covariate case C1, cases
ő≥
\gamma
‚ąą
\in
{
0
,
.8
}
\{0,.8\}
.
5.3.1
Ave-test
The ave-test shows size distortions in several cases: over-sized with strong
mutual dependence across covariates
ő≥
\gamma
=
=
.8
.8
at
n
n
=
=
100
100
, and
well under-sized as
p
n
p_{n}
increases under mutually independent covariates.
Covariate dependence extends to estimator dependence, which improves the
ave-test‚Äôs ability to detect deviations, especially for moderate to large
values of
p
n
p_{n}
. In most deviations from the null the ave-test is
(potentially massively) trumped by both the max-test and ART, while the
ave-test especially struggles with mutually independent covariates and/or
large dimension
p
n
p_{n}
.
The ave-test is especially vulnerable to high dimension. In multiple cases
power falls heavily as
p
n
p_{n}
increases compared to the max-test and ART.
Ultimately the reason is a max-statistic and ART work parsimoniously with
the single most informative (weighted) regression slope, while the
ave-statistic smooths over many possibly redundant or statistically
uninformative estimators (e.g. Models
i
‚Äč
i
,
ii,
i
‚Äč
i
‚Äč
i
,
iii,
i
‚Äč
v
iv
). The latter
significantly augments noise in the test, appearing as low empirical power
under high dimension. For example, in Model
i
‚Äč
i
‚Äč
i
iii
with mutually independent
covariates (
ő≥
\gamma
=
=
0
) and
n
n
‚ąą
\in
{
100
,
200
}
\{100,200\}
, empirical power
is roughly
5
%
5\%
for large
p
n
p_{n}
, yielding zero net power. See, e.g.,
Figure G.6, first and third column.
The only case where the ave-test may have an advantage is under Model
v
v
with a weak dense deviation from the null (in certain error and covariate
cases), as intuited by
Zhang and Laber (
2015
)
. For example, under a very weak
signal
ŌÜ
\varphi
=
=
.10
.10
with
n
n
‚ąą
\in
{
200
,
400
}
\{200,400\}
and covariate
mutual independence
ő≥
\gamma
=
=
0
, empirical power rises faster and over
higher dimensions. The result is more attenuated with a stronger signal
ŌÜ
\varphi
=
=
.15
.15
and greater noise caused by GARCH innovations (see
Figure G.8, third row, third column). Overall, however, the ave-test is
either roughly equivalent to or dominated by the max-test and ART.
5.3.2
Max-test and ART
We finish this section by focusing on the max-test and ART. In general these
tests perform better with covariate dependence and limited error volatility
(i.e. iid shocks), where neither is surprising. Empirical power for all
tests plunge with GARCH volatility, and increases in
n
n
. In many cases the
ART and max-test are quite similar in their responses to covariate
dependence, error properties, and degree of signal or deviation from the
null, in particular at
n
n
=
=
400
400
.
A few notable differences, however, do exist. Overall the max-test is best
suited for detecting subtle deviations from the null (weak signal), matching
evidence elsewhere
(e.g. Hill and Li,
2025
; Hill,
2025b
)
. This is
best revealed by Model
i
‚Äč
i
ii
(one relevant covariate) with weak signal
Ōē
1
\phi_{1}
=
=
.15
.15
, and Model
i
‚Äč
v
iv
(one relevant AR covariate) with
ŌÜ
\varphi
=
=
.25
.25
. See Figures G.2, G.3, G.7 and G.8. In Model
i
‚Äč
i
ii
with mutually
independent covariates the max-test dominates, and is dominant even under
mutual dependence (
ő≥
\gamma
=
=
.8
.8
) when the signal is very weak (
Ōē
1
\phi_{1}
=
=
.15
.15
), or with GARCH errors and independent covariates when the
signal is stronger (
Ōē
1
\phi_{1}
=
=
.25
.25
). In Model
i
‚Äč
v
iv
with a weak signal
ŌÜ
\varphi
=
=
.25
.25
the max-test strongly dominates at smaller sample sizes
n
n
‚ąą
\in
{
100
,
200
}
\{100,200\}
, an advantage that all but disappears at
n
n
=
=
400
400
or with a stronger signal
ŌÜ
\varphi
=
=
.50
.50
.
Thus, the ART procedure and its reliance on the most informative covariate
index
ńĪ
^
n
\hat{\imath}_{n}
has real merit, as revealed in
McKeague and Qian (
2015
)
, subsequent comments in, e.g.,
Zhang and Laber (
2015
)
,
and our study. That said, considering that ART is a type of max-test,
why
ART works well in some cases and less-so in others must be due
to its use and control of
ńĪ
^
n
\hat{\imath}_{n}
in a bootstrap setting akin to
ideas in
Andrews and Cheng (
2012
)
and
Hill (
2021
)
. That ART and its
bootstrap theory operate on a stochastic index
ńĪ
^
n
\hat{\imath}_{n}
adds
complexity to the procedure and limit theory by introducing weak
identification, even under the simulation refinement in
Zhang and Laber (
2015
)
. In this paper we only operate on the max- or ave-statistic with a wild
bootstrap, never requiring the optimal covariate index. The streamlined
procedure leads to size control, and dominant or competitive power for the
max-test, in particular against very weak and sparse deviations from the
null.
6
Conclusion
We present block bootstrap max- and ave-tests for detecting the presence of
significant predictors in a high dimensional setting. The number of
covariates to be screened may be large
p
p
>>
>>
n
n
, growing at an
exponential rate, and we allow for weakly dependent and heterogeneous
(possibly non-stationary) data. Our theory gives social and material
scientists broad access to cross-sections, time series and panels where high
dimensions are frequently encountered. The work here should extend with
moderate effort to coherence estimation under mutual and serial dependence
in high dimensional settings. Another interesting prospect is to extend the
optimal test class in
Andrews and
Ploberger (
1994
)
to the HD setting here,
where the nuisance term is the covariate index
i
i
. The goal here will be to
place HD max- and ave-tests properly within a field of optimal tests in the
sense of greatest weighted average power. We leave these ideas for future
work.

Instructions for reporting errors
We are continuing to improve HTML versions of papers, and your feedback helps enhance accessibility and mobile
      support. To report errors in the HTML that will help us improve conversion and rendering, choose any of the
      methods listed below:
Click the "Report Issue"
(
)
button, located in the page header.
Tip:
You can select the relevant text first, to include it in your report.
Our team has already identified
the following issues
. We appreciate your time reviewing and reporting rendering errors we
      may not have found yet. Your efforts will help us improve the HTML versions for all readers, because disability
      should not be a barrier to accessing research. Thank you for your continued support in championing open access for
      all.
Have a free development cycle? Help support accessibility at arXiv! Our collaborators at LaTeXML maintain a
list of packages that need conversion
, and welcome
developer contributions
.
